The problem with current version: It resamples twice (once to change pitch, then back to original length), which cancels out the effect!
Now let me address your other excellent questions:
ANALYSIS OF YOUR QUESTIONS:
1. Robot Effect - Probability/Segment Control:
You're absolutely right! Robot shouldn't be 100% of the time. Looking at the old app, it was continuous, but for realism we need:
	‚Ä¢	Robot intensity¬†(how strong when it occurs)
	‚Ä¢	Robot probability¬†(how often it occurs per segment)
2. Stutter vs Jitter vs Packet Loss vs Buffering:
These are different artifacts:
	‚Ä¢	Stutter: Cellular dropouts/signal loss (what we have)
	‚Ä¢	Jitter: VoIP timing variations, buffering delays
	‚Ä¢	Packet Loss: Missing audio chunks (similar to dropouts but different pattern)
	‚Ä¢	Buffering: VoIP rebuffering artifacts, smearing
We should include these when we split into cellular/landline/VoIP categories.
3. Event Sample Logic - File Length Threshold:
Excellent point! For the car horns/traffic:
	‚Ä¢	Short files (<3 seconds): Play fully, random placement
	‚Ä¢	Long files (‚â•3 seconds): Random start point, can loop, treat as ambient
A 3-second threshold makes perfect sense - short enough for "one-shot" sounds, long enough for ambient scenes.

	1	Fix garble with EXACT old implementation¬†first
	2	Add robot probability control
	3	Implement smart event file handling¬†with 3-second threshold

Add small pitch/time warp on reinserted frames to emulate bad PLC.‚Ä®‚Ä¢ Time-domain glitching: Chop audio into 10‚Äì200 ms grains and randomly reorder or repeat for stutter effects. Perfect! Now I can see the complete picture. Here's the analysis:
üéØ Current System: Mixed Approach
‚úÖ Effects That ARE Multiplied by Quality Tier:
	‚Ä¢	Garble:¬†garble_prob √ó garble_mult¬†(tier-dependent)
	‚Ä¢	Dropouts:¬†dropout_prob √ó dropout_mult¬†(tier-dependent)
‚úÖ Effects That Are GLOBAL (Not Multiplied):
	‚Ä¢	Stutter: Direct slider value
	‚Ä¢	Jitter/Buffering: Direct slider values
	‚Ä¢	Packet Reordering: Direct slider value
	‚Ä¢	Codec Artifacts: Direct slider value
	‚Ä¢	Mic Proximity: Direct slider value
	‚Ä¢	MP3 Sizzle: Direct slider value
	‚Ä¢	RF Noise: Direct slider value
ü§î Which Approach Is Better?
Option A: Keep Current Mixed System
Pros:
	‚Ä¢	Garble/Dropouts scale realistically¬†with connection quality
	‚Ä¢	Other effects stay consistent¬†across quality tiers
	‚Ä¢	Intuitive: Bad connections = more garble/dropouts, but jitter is separate
Cons:
	‚Ä¢	Inconsistent behavior¬†- some sliders scale, others don't
	‚Ä¢	Confusing for users¬†- hard to predict what will happen
Option B: Make All Effects Global (No Multiplication)
Pros:
	‚Ä¢	Consistent behavior¬†- all sliders work the same way
	‚Ä¢	Predictable¬†- slider value = actual effect amount
	‚Ä¢	Simpler presets¬†- what you set is what you get
Cons:
	‚Ä¢	Less realistic¬†- bad connections should have more artifacts
	‚Ä¢	More manual work¬†- need to set each effect individually per quality
Option C: Make All Effects Scale with Quality
Pros:
	‚Ä¢	Most realistic¬†- all artifacts scale with connection quality
	‚Ä¢	Automatic coherence¬†- bad quality = more of everything
	‚Ä¢	Easier presets¬†- just pick quality tier + base amounts
Cons:
	‚Ä¢	Less granular control¬†- can't have "bad connection but no jitter"
	‚Ä¢	Complex to understand¬†- all sliders are relative
üéØ My Recommendation: Option B (All Global)
For your three presets for Bojan, I'd recommend making all effects global because:
	1	Predictable for clients¬†- they adjust a slider, they hear the change
	2	Easier to create presets¬†- you set exact values that work
	3	More flexible¬†- can create "good connection in bad environment" vs "bad connection"
	4	Simpler to explain¬†- "This slider controls garble amount, period"
Quality tiers would then focus on:
	‚Ä¢	Bandwidth filtering
	‚Ä¢	Compression artifacts
	‚Ä¢	Background noise
	‚Ä¢	Sample rate simulation
Network artifacts would be separate creative controls.

üéØ Landline-Specific Degradation Types

	‚ó¶	Crackles/pops: Physical line issues

	‚Ä¢	RF-specific issues:

Here‚Äôs how I‚Äôd shape those three presets inside your FX engine. Think of them as bundles of codec, IR, and artifact settings that mimic what people expect from ‚Äúgood landline,‚Äù ‚Äúbad landline,‚Äù and ‚Äúcordless.‚Äù

‚úÖ Preset Recipes
1. Good Landline (POTS, clear)

Codec type: G.711 ¬µ-law or AMR-WB (wideband)

Bandwidth: 300‚Äì3400 Hz (narrowband)

Bitrate: ~64 kbps

Artifacts:

Dropout prob: 0

Jitter intensity: 0

Packet reorder: 0

IR: Handset IR with modest EQ tilt (slight low roll-off, 3‚Äì4 kHz ceiling)

Noise: None or very subtle hiss

Status string: ‚ÄúOK ‚Äì Clean PSTN line, standard call center quality‚Äù

2. Bad Landline (noisy analog loop)

Codec type: G.711 ¬µ-law

Bandwidth: 300‚Äì3000 Hz

Bitrate: ~64 kbps

Artifacts:

Dropout prob: 0.05‚Äì0.1

Jitter: light (0.01‚Äì0.02)

Packet reorder: occasional (0.01)

Noise: Add steady RF hum or 60 Hz buzz layer, + a hiss bed at -28 dB

Distortion: Subtle harmonic distortion on loud peaks

IR: Narrow, slightly resonant handset IR

Status string: ‚ÄúPoor loop ‚Äì Crosstalk, hiss, slight crackle‚Äù

3. Cordless Phone (2.4 GHz / 5.8 GHz era)

Codec type: G.726 ADPCM (32 kbps) or simulate with Opus @ 16‚Äì24 kbps

Bandwidth: 300‚Äì3400 Hz, but add steep high-cut at 2.8‚Äì3 kHz

Artifacts:

Dropout prob: 0.05‚Äì0.15 (simulate range interference)

Stutter: 0.02‚Äì0.04 (repetition clicks)

Buffer smears: 50‚Äì100 ms

Noise: RF chirps or short digital ‚Äúbursts‚Äù (simulate cordless interference)

IR: Handset IR (plastic shell resonance)

Status string: ‚ÄúCordless ‚Äì Range artifacts, clipped highs, RF pops‚Äù

üéØ Extended Notes

These three can all share the same ‚ÄúHandset IR‚Äù slot ‚Äì the differentiation is codec + artifact + noise layering.

For cordless, the artifacts should be dynamic (short bursts, not continuous), so tie dropout + stutter to a seeded RNG for variation.

‚ÄúBad landline‚Äù should feel steady bad (always hissy, always dull), while ‚Äúcordless‚Äù should feel intermittently bad (good one second, glitchy the next).

Short answer: Yes. Even if the RF Noise slider is at 0, some quality tiers inject their own ‚Äúbase‚Äù noise via the tier‚Äôs noise_level. That tier noise is separate from (and in addition to) the RF Noise slider.

	‚Ä¢	Each tier defines noise_level
	‚Ä¢	The phone quality processor always adds that base noise if noise_level > 0
Tier definitions (excerpt):



app.py































Where it‚Äôs applied:



app.py

























RF Noise slider path (separate from tier noise):
	‚Ä¢	RF slider feeds apply_rf_noise_old; at 0 it adds nothing.



app.py




























What this means in practice
	‚Ä¢	With RF Noise slider at 0, you can still hear hiss/static if the selected tier has noise_level > 0 (e.g., bad_landline, cordless, ultra_low, low, standard).
	‚Ä¢	Tiers with no base noise: good_landline, high, ultra_high (noise_level = 0.0).
How to get a truly clean baseline
	‚Ä¢	Choose a tier with noise_level = 0.0: good_landline, high, ultra_high; or use ‚Äúcustom‚Äù and set Custom Noise to 0.0.
	‚Ä¢	Keep RF Noise slider at 0.
	‚Ä¢	Also ensure MP3 Sizzle is 0 and background/IR levels are 0 if you want silence beyond the voice.

Short answer
	‚Ä¢	Yes, you can make ‚Äúmore than 100%‚Äù dereverb a single slider‚Äîno separate button needed. Just map slider values above 1.0 to additional internal passes.
	‚Ä¢	Your current ‚Äúdereverb‚Äù is spectral noise reduction (noisereduce). It‚Äôs great for hiss/ambience, but it‚Äôs not true dereverberation. If you want more accurate de-reverb (reduce late reflections), consider WPE (Weighted Prediction Error).
What you have now (from code)
	‚Ä¢	You‚Äôre using noisereduce non‚Äëstationary spectral gating with prop_decrease clamped to [0,1].
	‚Ä¢	Slider in UI is 0.0‚Äì1.0; default 0.35.



app.py



























Can we extend the slider range without a button?
	‚Ä¢	Yes. Noisereduce‚Äôs prop_decrease tops out at 1.0 (100%) [docs: ‚ÄúThe proportion to reduce the noise by (1.0 = 100%)‚Äù]. You can interpret slider >1.0 as ‚Äúrun another pass‚Äù under the hood. Example mapping:
	‚ó¶	0.0‚Äì1.0 ‚Üí one pass with prop_decrease = value
	‚ó¶	1.0‚Äì2.0 ‚Üí two passes: first at 1.0, second at value‚àí1.0
	‚ó¶	2.0‚Äì3.0 ‚Üí three passes, etc. This gives a single control with more range, avoiding a ‚ÄúSuper‚Äù button.
Minimal example (how to map without changing user-visible behavior)





























Are there better algorithms for dereverb?
	‚Ä¢	For real dereverberation (reducing late reflections), WPE is the classic, lightweight choice:
	‚ó¶	NARA‚ÄëWPE (Python) is a standard implementation (offline/online). It targets reverberation, not hiss.
	‚ó¶	References:
	‚ñ™	NARA‚ÄëWPE package:¬†https://github.com/fgnt/nara_wpe
	‚ñ™	Paper: ‚ÄúNARA‚ÄëWPE: A Python package for weighted prediction error dereverberation‚Ä¶‚Äù
	‚Ä¢	Trade‚Äëoffs:
	‚ó¶	Noisereduce (what you use) = spectral gating denoiser; strong settings can suppress ambience but can leave ‚Äúgating/musical noise‚Äù and is less effective on reverb tails.
	‚ó¶	WPE = dereverberation; better at late reflections, tends to preserve timbre; moderate CPU, works at 16 kHz; widely used in ASR front‚Äëends.
	‚ó¶	Deep models (Demucs variants, DCCRN, DeepFilterNet2) can do both denoise + dereverb but are heavier and introduce dependency/latency. For radio production, WPE often gives a clean, realistic ‚Äúcloser mic‚Äù feel without heavy artifacts.
Recommendation
	‚Ä¢	If you want to keep your current stack and just give ‚Äúmore‚Äù on one control: extend the slider range and map >1.0 to extra noisereduce passes (no button needed).
	‚Ä¢	If you want results that are more faithful to ‚Äúdereverb‚Äù (reduce room tail while keeping presence), consider adding a WPE option next (keep noisereduce for hiss).
Citations
	‚Ä¢	Noisereduce (prop_decrease up to 1.0): GitHub README, ‚ÄúThe proportion to reduce the noise by (1.0 = 100%)‚Äù¬†https://github.com/timsainb/noisereduce
	‚Ä¢	WPE dereverberation (NARA‚ÄëWPE):¬†https://github.com/fgnt/nara_wpe¬†(Python package and docs)
		‚Ä®‚Ä®
		‚Ä®‚Ä®

lets use wpe as well, are you saying to re label the current de reverb as noise reduce?

Web Fetch




Short answer
	‚Ä¢	Yes: I recommend relabeling the current control to ‚ÄúNoise Reduction (Spectral Gate)‚Äù and adding a separate ‚ÄúDereverberation (WPE)‚Äù control.
	‚Ä¢	Reason: your current slider drives noisereduce‚Äôs spectral gating (great for hiss/ambience), while WPE is the standard algorithm for removing late reverberation. Keeping them distinct makes the UI accurate and avoids confusion.
What you have now (confirmed)
	‚Ä¢	The ‚ÄúReduce Reverb (strong)‚Äù slider calls noisereduce.reduce_noise with prop_decrease ‚àà [0,1]. That is a denoiser, not a true dereverberation method.
	‚Ä¢	Reference: noisereduce docs describe prop_decrease as ‚ÄúThe proportion to reduce the noise by (1.0 = 100%).‚Äù
Why add WPE
	‚Ä¢	WPE (Weighted Prediction Error) is a classic dereverberation algorithm (Yoshioka & Nakatani, 2012) specifically targeting late reflections.
	‚Ä¢	NARA‚ÄëWPE is a solid Python package (MIT) widely used in ASR front-ends and research:
	‚ó¶	GitHub: fgnt/nara_wpe
	‚ó¶	Docs: nara-wpe.readthedocs.io
	‚Ä¢	It‚Äôs most effective on multi-mic audio but can still help single-channel; if you later want stronger mono performance, DNN‚ÄëWPE variants exist.
How to present both in the UI (clear and accurate)
	‚Ä¢	Noise Reduction (Spectral Gate)
	‚ó¶	Algorithm: noisereduce (stationary=False)
	‚ó¶	Range: 0.0‚Äì1.0 for a single pass; if you want ‚Äúmore than 100%,‚Äù you can map values >1.0 to additional passes internally (no separate ‚Äúsuper‚Äù button needed).
	‚Ä¢	Dereverberation (WPE)
	‚ó¶	Algorithm: NARA‚ÄëWPE
	‚ó¶	Typical controls (keep simple for internal team):
	‚ñ™	Strength (iterations): 0‚Äì3
	‚ñ™	Prediction order (taps): ~10‚Äì20
	‚ñ™	Delay (frames): ~3‚Äì5
	‚ó¶	Defaults: Strength=1‚Äì2, Order=10‚Äì16, Delay=3 are common starting points at 16 kHz.

	‚ó¶	Package: NARA‚ÄëWPE (MIT)¬†https://github.com/fgnt/nara_wpe, docs:¬†https://nara-wpe.readthedocs.io/en/latest/

12:38 PM





just revisit this list by gpt, see if you agree, and if we are following similar settings - ‚úÖ Operational Layer ‚Äî Ranked degradations (worst ‚Üí best)

Heavily dropped 2G / edge-case GSM with severe packet loss

Cause: roaming on old 2G towers, extreme congestion, or weak signal with codec retries.

Audible signs: frequent micro-dropouts, heavy warbling, gating, stuttering, time-domain glitches, severe codec artifacts (chirps, metallic warble).

Bandwidth: effectively intermittent narrowband - often 3.5‚Äì4 kHz usable, but fragmented.

Quick fixes: aggressive packet-loss concealment, short look-ahead crossfades, transient smoothing, gating to hide gaps, avoid airing long takes. If possible, ask caller to move to a stable spot.

Cellphone on 2G/3G with codec clipping and intermittent digital artifacts

Cause: legacy codecs (AMR-NB), poor handoff between cells, codec resync artifacts.

Audible signs: narrow bandwidth, metallic harshness, occasional digital pops and transient distortion.

Bandwidth: 3.5‚Äì4 kHz (narrowband).

Quick fixes: low-pass at ~4 kHz, gentle de-essing, mild harmonic enhancer if intelligibility lost, tighten compressors with slow release to smooth pumping.

4G LTE variable with heavy jitter and packet loss (VoLTE unstable)

Cause: mobile network congestion, handoffs, or poor cellular data path causing UDP packet loss.

Audible signs: micro-clipping, repeated syllables, moments of scrambled syllables or pitch-shift warble.

Bandwidth: can be wideband (up to 16 kHz) but effectively reduced during loss.

Quick fixes: jitter buffer + PLC (packet loss conceal), transient repair, short spectral fill to mask missing HF content. Consider a mild multi-band expander to tame background bursts.

VoIP mobile app over poor Wi-Fi (WhatsApp/Telegram over congested Wi-Fi)

Cause: consumer VoIP over NAT, weak Wi-Fi, client-side low bitrate, background apps.

Audible signs: fluttering, occasional rebuffering smears, narrow HF, variable latency.

Bandwidth: often wideband if conditions allow, otherwise limited 8‚Äì16 kHz.

Quick fixes: increase jitter buffer, use narrow low-pass to stabilize, apply gentle de-reverb if room echo present.

Cellphone on good LTE / stable VoLTE

Cause: decent network, stable data path.

Audible signs: fairly stable, some compression artifacts on plosives and sibilance, limited low/sub-bass.

Bandwidth: often wideband 7‚Äì16 kHz depending on carrier and handset.

Quick fixes: remove low rumble (<100 Hz), subtle multiband compression, transient control, de-esser.

App-based high-quality VoIP (FaceTime Audio, WhatsApp, Telegram) on good Wi-Fi or LTE

Cause: app uses wideband Opus/ALAC-ish paths; FaceTime uses AAC-ELD-like quality when conditions are good.

Audible signs: clear voice, modest compression, low latency, minimal artifacts.

Bandwidth: near CD-ish in ideal conditions - 16 kHz+ effective.

Quick fixes: very light EQ, mild leveling, minimal gating. Preserve natural tone.

Station smartphone answered locally then routed via a clean studio input (analog/digital DI)

Cause: caller uses smartphone, studio runs it through a clean mix-minus on a high-quality preamp.

Audible signs: natural but slightly limited low end from the phone mic; no network glitches.

Bandwidth: depends on phone mic - wideband but microphone-limited 100 Hz‚Äì10 kHz typical.

Quick fixes: EQ to add presence and low roll-off compensation, broadband compression, de-noise low-end hum.

Dedicated IP codec / studio-grade app (Comrex, Telos, Source-Connect etc.)

Cause: caller connected through a professional IP codec or studio sends/receives via managed network path.

Audible signs: consistent latency, high intelligibility, broadcast-safe dynamics.

Bandwidth: full broadcast quality 48 kHz or at least 16‚Äì24 kHz.

Quick fixes: standard broadcast chain - HPF, multiband comp, limiter, subtle EQ.

Clean landline (PSTN)

Cause: stable PSTN connection, analog telephone lines passed through quality hybrid.

Audible signs: consistent but narrowband timbre - intelligible but boxy.

Bandwidth: narrowband ~3.4‚Äì4 kHz.

Quick fixes: shelving EQ to brighten midrange, aggressive de-essing avoided, clarity via presence boost 2‚Äì4 kHz.

FaceTime Audio / WhatsApp / Telegram on great connection (best consumer case)

Cause: modern codecs (Opus/AAC) + clean network + good handset mic.

Audible signs: smooth, high intelligibility, low noise, near-studio clarity for voice.

Bandwidth: near-wideband to super-wide (16‚Äì20 kHz equiv for FaceTime in good conditions).

Quick fixes: minimal - light leveling and maybe a subtle low-cut.

Studio-grade remote feed - high quality (ISDN-era equivalent via IP)

Cause: professional two-way codec over dedicated ISP with QoS.

Audible signs: near-studio parity, reliable timing, broadcast-ready.

Bandwidth: 48 kHz and up.

Quick fixes: standard broadcast chain only.

üéØ Contextual Layer ‚Äî short analysis and practical notes

Yes, listeners can realistically use FaceTime Audio or WhatsApp/Telegram to call a station, but there are caveats. FaceTime Audio requires both caller and recipient to be on Apple devices. WhatsApp/Telegram calls work across platforms. Practically, most stations do not connect these services directly into their on-air chain because:

They lack a stable, recordable, and mix-minus-friendly SIP interface.

Recording and consent metadata are harder to capture.

Latency, jitter, and NAT traversal can be unpredictable.

Workarounds stations use:

Answer on a dedicated smartphone logged into FaceTime/WhatsApp and patch that phone into the board via a high-quality hybrid or audio interface. This is the simplest and most common ad-hoc approach.

Bridge/convert the mobile VoIP call to SIP/PSTN using a gateway service so it can be handled by studio codecs/hybrids.

Use a dedicated app or remote codec (Comrex Access, Telos Axia xNode, CleanFeed, Source-Connect) for higher reliability and professional audio.

If you want to design the Voice Lab FX engine to handle this range:

Add automatic routing presets by source type (narrowband mobile, VoIP, studio codec).

Auto-detect bandwidth (estimate via spectral analysis) and apply matched processing chains.

Include PLC and jitter-buffer aware smoothing modules for VoIP, plus IR-based phone-speaker simulation for creative presets.

Quick practical recipe for a station accepting app calls

Step 1: Dedicate a smartphone per service (FaceTime, WhatsApp) and connect to a clean preamp or hybrid.

Step 2: Route into a channel with a Mix-Minus, apply an on-the-fly preset chosen from the ranked list above.

Step 3: Record the channel (for compliance), and monitor latency; if latency > ~250 ms consider using the caller for short recorded drops, not live back-and-forth.

Step 4: If you expect frequent VoIP callers, invest in a SIP/VoIP gateway that can convert common app calls into SIP or PSTN for studio-grade handling.

If you want, I can:

give you a compact presets.json with recommended EQ/compressor settings per call type, or

sketch a routing diagram for patching smartphones into your console.

gpt said this, do you agree? - illing Œº-law across landline tiers is a little blunt. It fixes the crunch, but you lose the ‚Äúthat‚Äôs a phone‚Äù fingerprint. Classic PSTN vibe comes more from companding and quantization than from bandwidth alone.‚Ä®‚Ä®‚úÖ Operational layer ‚Äî keep realism without re-introducing distortion‚Ä®‚Ä®Best compromise‚Ä®‚Ä®Keep Œº-law, but only on the remote voice band and in parallel at a low amount.‚Ä®‚Ä®Pre-limit the remote mix, then split the band so Œº-law bends mostly sibilants and mids, not the whole bed.‚Ä®‚Ä®Target defaults‚Ä®‚Ä®good_landline: amount 0.20, voice band 300‚Äì2400 Hz, drive 0.70‚Ä®‚Ä®bad_landline: amount 0.30, voice band 300‚Äì2200 Hz, drive 0.65‚Ä®‚Ä®cordless: amount 0.15, voice band 300‚Äì2600 Hz, drive 0.75‚Ä®‚Ä®high and up: amount 0.00‚Ä®‚Ä®Minimal patch pattern‚Ä®‚Ä®# before phone tier‚Ä®remote_mix = _prelimit(remote_mix, 0.707)‚Ä®‚Ä®# inside apply_phone_quality_tier(...)‚Ä®def _mulaw_color(x, amount, mu=255.0, drive=0.75):‚Ä®if amount <= 0.0: return x.astype(np.float32)‚Ä®x = _prelimit(x, 0.707)‚Ä®y = np.sign(x) * np.log1p(mu * np.abs(np.clip(x*drive, -1, 1))) / np.log1p(mu)‚Ä®# decode pass to make it ‚Äúcodec color‚Äù not harsh nonlinearity‚Ä®y = np.sign(y) * (np.expm1(np.log1p(mu) * np.abs(y)) / mu)‚Ä®out = (1.0 - amount) * x + amount * y‚Ä®return _soft_clip(out, 1.02).astype(np.float32)‚Ä®‚Ä®def _band(x, lo, hi):‚Ä®return _zphf(x, hpf_hz=lo, lpf_hz=hi, sr=SR)‚Ä®‚Ä®# landline tiers only‚Ä®if tier in ("good_landline","bad_landline","cordless"):‚Ä®amt = {"good_landline":0.20,"bad_landline":0.30,"cordless":0.15}[tier]‚Ä®vb_lo, vb_hi = {"good_landline":(300,2400),‚Ä®"bad_landline":(300,2200),‚Ä®"cordless":(300,2600)}[tier]‚Ä®vband = _band(y, vb_lo, vb_hi)‚Ä®rest = y - vband‚Ä®vband = _mulaw_color(vband, amt, mu=255.0, drive={"good_landline":0.70,"bad_landline":0.65,"cordless":0.75}[tier])‚Ä®y = vband + rest‚Ä®‚Ä®‚Ä®Safety rails already in your build still matter‚Ä®‚Ä®Keep your pre-limit on the combined remote mix.‚Ä®‚Ä®Keep zero-phase LPF and soft-clip on the bed generation.‚Ä®‚Ä®Keep overlap-aware jitter buffer and remove packet reordering from analog paths.‚Ä®‚Ä®UI toggle‚Ä®‚Ä®Add a simple switch: PSTN color [on/off]. üéØ How It Works:

	2	Apply Œº-law coloration¬†only to the voice band (not full spectrum)

	4	Recombine¬†with untouched frequencies above/below voice band

read all of this its gpt5 and gpt 5 codex talking - heres what gpt5 codex said - Here‚Äôs what still forces the ‚Äútelephone‚Äù tone, even on the ultra-high tier:

dropout_prob/garble_prob are auto-repopulated every time the tier is set to ultra_high (app.py:1227‚Äì1249). The handler hardwires a base dropout of 0.10 and garble of 0.02 before multiplying by the tier boosts, so ultra_high ends up at ~0.02 dropouts and 0.004 garble. Because process_audio always runs those artifacts (app.py:1008‚Äì1018), you‚Äôre guaranteed low-level dropouts/warbles even when everything else is ‚Äúoff‚Äù. That alone keeps the mix sounding constrained. Consider special-casing the handler to zero those baselines for modern tiers or skipping the artifact block entirely when the multipliers are zero.

The ‚ÄúPremium VoIP‚Äù preset the team has been testing is still pre-shaping the source before the tier: dereverb 0.8 and leveler 0.5 are baked into the preset defaults (app.py:1083‚Äì1088). Stage 1 of process_audio applies both unconditionally (app.py:906‚Äì915), so the voice hits heavy dereverb plus a tanh-driven leveler before it ever reaches the ultra_high chain. That combo shaves transients and ‚Äúair,‚Äù making the result feel band-limited even though the tier allows 20‚ÄØHz‚Äì20‚ÄØkHz. If you want near-source fidelity, you‚Äôll need either a cleaner preset or per-tier logic that bypasses this cleanup when you‚Äôre in a modern tier.

Even the new ‚ÄúüéØ True Source‚Äù preset can‚Äôt escape the artifact injection: although the preset sets dropouts/garble to zero (app.py:1092‚Äì1099), selecting it flips the tier to ultra_high, which immediately fires the change handler mentioned above and reintroduces the non-zero defaults. Users have to manually zero the sliders after picking the tier, otherwise the preset still sounds processed.

Quick diagnostic next steps:

Manually set dropout_prob = 0, garble_prob = 0, and stutter_amt = 0 after choosing ultra_high; rerun to confirm the high-end opens up.

fixes (minimal + safe)

@@ def on_quality_tier_change(tier, ...):

- base_garble = 0.02
+ # Modern tiers should be artifact-free by default
+ is_modern = tier in ("high","ultra_high")
+ base_dropout = 0.0 if is_modern else 0.10
+ base_garble = 0.0 if is_modern else 0.02

- dropout = base_dropout * tier_params.get("dropout_boost", 1.0)
- garble = base_garble * tier_params.get("garble_boost", 1.0)
+ dropout = (base_dropout * tier_params.get("dropout_boost", 1.0))
+ garble = (base_garble * tier_params.get("garble_boost", 1.0))

+ if dropdown_value_from_preset == 0: dropout = 0.0
+ if garble_value_from_preset == 0: garble = 0.0


(If you don‚Äôt have those *_from_preset values, just keep the modern-tier zeroing. That alone fixes the auto-repopulation issue.)

2) Stage-1 cleanup should bypass for modern tiers (or be preset-controllable)

Only apply dereverb/leveler pre-tier if the tier is not modern, or if the preset explicitly asked for it.

@@ def process_audio(...):
- y = apply_dereverb(y, dereverb_amt)
- y = apply_leveler(y, leveler_amt)
+ is_modern = quality_tier in ("high","ultra_high")
+ if not is_modern or (dereverb_amt or leveler_amt) > 0:
+ # honor explicit non-zero requests; otherwise skip for modern
+ if dereverb_amt > 0: y = apply_dereverb(y, dereverb_amt)
+ if leveler_amt > 0: y = apply_leveler(y, leveler_amt)

@@ def apply_codec_and_artifacts(x, dropout_prob, garble_intensity, stutter_amt, jitter_intensity, ...):
- # ... always applies stages, each checks its own amount ...
+ if max(dropout_prob, garble_intensity, stutter_amt, jitter_intensity) == 0.0:
+ return x
+ # else run the chain as before

05:10 PM





im having gpt codex try to fix it, they said i should have been killing and restarting the app not just refreshing because its python, thoughts?

GPT Codex is absolutely correct! This is a crucial point about Python applications that many people miss.
üéØ Why Restarting vs Refreshing Matters:
Browser Refresh (What you were doing):
	‚Ä¢	Only reloads the¬†web interface¬†(HTML/CSS/JavaScript)
	‚Ä¢	The¬†Python process keeps running¬†with the same code in memory
	‚Ä¢	Code changes are NOT reflected¬†until the Python process restarts
App Restart (What you should do):
	‚Ä¢	Kills the Python process¬†and starts fresh
	‚Ä¢	Reloads all Python code¬†from disk
	‚Ä¢	All imports, variables, and functions¬†get the updated versions
üéØ This Explains Everything:
Your symptoms:
	‚Ä¢	Made code changes to fix Stage-1 bypass logic
	‚Ä¢	Refreshed browser but still heard telephone sound
	‚Ä¢	Output analysis showed 16kHz instead of 48kHz
What was happening:
	‚Ä¢	The¬†old Python code¬†was still running in memory
	‚Ä¢	Your¬†SR = 48000¬†change never took effect
	‚Ä¢	The¬†Stage-1 bypass fixes¬†never took effect
	‚Ä¢	The app was still using the¬†old logic with SR = 16000
üéØ How to Properly Restart:
	1	Kill the Python process:
	‚ó¶	Press¬†Ctrl+C¬†in the terminal running the app
	‚ó¶	Or close the terminal entirely
	2	Restart the app:
	‚ó¶	Run¬†python app.py¬†again
	‚ó¶	Wait for "Running on local URL" message
	3	Then¬†open/refresh the browser
üéØ This is Why:
	‚Ä¢	Python imports modules once¬†and caches them
	‚Ä¢	Global variables¬†(like¬†SR = 48000) are set at import time
	‚Ä¢	Function definitions¬†are loaded into memory at startup
	‚Ä¢	Gradio interface¬†is built once when the app starts
Browser refresh ‚â† Python restart
GPT Codex is spot-on - you need to kill and restart the Python process to see your code changes. This is probably why all the fixes seemed to have no effect!
Try restarting the app now and test ultra_high again.
		‚Ä®‚Ä®
